<p><b>Iceland</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="noexcerpt"><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e0/En-us-Iceland.ogg" title="Listen"><img alt="Listen" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/11px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/17px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/22px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="11" data-file-height="11" /></a><sup><span class="IPA" style="color:#00e;font:bold 80% sans-serif;padding:0 .1em"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Iceland.ogg" title="File:En-us-Iceland.ogg">i</a></span></sup></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/aɪ/ long 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>: <span lang="is"><i>Ísland</i></span> <small>pronounced:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Icelandic" title="Help:IPA for Icelandic">[ˈistlant]</a></span>),<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic</a> <a href="/wiki/Island_country" title="Island country">island country</a> in the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Ocean" class="mw-redirect" title="North Atlantic Ocean">North Atlantic Ocean</a>. It has a population of 332,529 and an area of 103,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (40,000&#160;sq&#160;mi), making it the most sparsely populated country in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Statice_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Statice-8">[8]</a></sup> The capital and largest city is <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk" title="Reykjavík">Reykjavík</a>. Reykjavík and the surrounding areas in the southwest of the country are home to over two-thirds of the population. Iceland is <a href="/wiki/High_island" title="High island">volcanically</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_(geology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Geothermal (geology)">geologically active</a>. The interior consists of a plateau characterised by sand and <a href="/wiki/Lava_field" title="Lava field">lava fields</a>, mountains and glaciers, while many <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">glacial rivers</a> flow to the sea through the lowlands. Iceland is warmed by the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a> and has a temperate climate, despite a high latitude just outside the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a>. Its high latitude and marine influence still keeps summers chilly, with most of the archipelago having a <a href="/wiki/Tundra_climate" class="mw-redirect" title="Tundra climate">tundra climate</a>.</p>
<p>According to <i><a href="/wiki/Landn%C3%A1mab%C3%B3k" title="Landnámabók">Landnámabók</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_Iceland" title="Settlement of Iceland">settlement of Iceland</a> began in the year 874 AD when the <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norwegian</a> chieftain <a href="/wiki/Ing%C3%B3lfr_Arnarson" title="Ingólfr Arnarson">Ingólfr Arnarson</a> became the first permanent settler on the island.<sup id="cite_ref-tomasson_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tomasson-9">[9]</a></sup> In the following centuries, <a href="/wiki/Norwegians" title="Norwegians">Norwegians</a>, and to a lesser extent other <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavians</a>, immigrated to Iceland, bringing with them <a href="/wiki/Thrall" title="Thrall">thralls</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> origin. The island was governed as an <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Commonwealth" title="Icelandic Commonwealth">independent commonwealth</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Althing" title="Althing">Althing</a>, one of the world's oldest functioning legislative assemblies. Following a <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Sturlungs" class="mw-redirect" title="Age of Sturlungs">period of civil strife</a>, Iceland acceded to Norwegian rule in the 13th century. The establishment of the Kalmar Union in 1397 united the kingdoms of Norway, Denmark and Sweden. Iceland thus followed Norway's integration to that Union and came under Danish rule after Sweden's secession from that union in 1523. Although the Danish kingdom introduced Lutheranism forcefully in 1550, Iceland remained a distant semi-colonial territory in which Danish institutions and infrastructures were conspicuous by their absence. In the wake of the French revolution and the Napoleonic wars, Iceland's struggle for independence took form and culminated in <a href="/wiki/Danish%E2%80%93Icelandic_Act_of_Union" title="Danish–Icelandic Act of Union">independence in 1918</a> and the founding of a <a href="/wiki/Founding_of_the_Republic_of_Iceland" title="Founding of the Republic of Iceland">republic in 1944</a>. Until the 20th century, Iceland relied largely on subsistence fishing and agriculture, and was among the poorest in Europe. Industrialisation of the fisheries and <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> aid following <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> brought prosperity, and Iceland became one of the wealthiest and most developed nations in the world. In 1994, it became a part of the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a>, which further diversified the economy into sectors such as finance, biotechnology, and manufacturing.</p>
<p>Iceland has a <a href="/wiki/Market_economy" title="Market economy">market economy</a> with relatively low taxes compared to other <a href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">OECD</a> countries.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> It maintains a <a href="/wiki/Nordic_model" title="Nordic model">Nordic social welfare system</a> that provides <a href="/wiki/Universal_health_care" title="Universal health care">universal health care</a> and tertiary education for its citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Iceland ranks high in economic, political and social stability and equality. In 2013, it was ranked as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Human_Development_Index" title="List of countries by Human Development Index">13th most-developed country</a> in the world by the <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">United Nations' Human Development Index</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HDI_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HDI-6">[6]</a></sup> Iceland runs almost completely on <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>. Affected by the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008" title="Financial crisis of 2007–2008">worldwide financial crisis</a>, the nation's entire banking system <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932011_Icelandic_financial_crisis" title="2008–2011 Icelandic financial crisis">systemically failed</a> in October 2008, leading to a <a href="/wiki/Depression_(economics)" title="Depression (economics)">severe depression</a>, substantial <a href="/wiki/2009_Icelandic_financial_crisis_protests" title="2009 Icelandic financial crisis protests">political unrest</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Icesave_dispute" title="Icesave dispute">Icesave dispute</a>, and the institution of <a href="/wiki/Capital_control" title="Capital control">capital controls</a>. Many bankers were jailed,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> and the economy has made a significant recovery, in large part due to a surge in <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Iceland" title="Tourism in Iceland">tourism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Iceland" title="Culture of Iceland">Icelandic culture</a> is founded upon the nation's Scandinavian heritage. Most Icelanders are descendants of <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gael" class="mw-redirect" title="Gael">Gaelic</a> settlers. <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>, a <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">North Germanic language</a>, is descended from <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> and is closely related to <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_dialects" title="Norwegian dialects">West Norwegian dialects</a>. The country's cultural heritage includes traditional <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_cuisine" title="Icelandic cuisine">Icelandic cuisine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_literature" title="Icelandic literature">Icelandic literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sagas_of_Icelanders" title="Sagas of Icelanders">medieval sagas</a>. Iceland has the smallest population of any <a href="/wiki/Member_states_of_NATO" title="Member states of NATO">NATO member</a> and is the only one with <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_without_armed_forces" title="List of countries without armed forces">no standing army</a>, its lightly armed <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Coast_Guard" title="Icelandic Coast Guard">coast guard</a> being in charge of defence.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iceland" title="History of Iceland">History of Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Icelandic_history" title="Timeline of Icelandic history">Timeline of Icelandic history</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Settlement_and_Commonwealth_874.E2.80.931262">Settlement and Commonwealth 874–1262</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Settlement and Commonwealth 874–1262">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Norsemen_Landing_in_Iceland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Norsemen_Landing_in_Iceland.jpg/220px-Norsemen_Landing_in_Iceland.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Norsemen_Landing_in_Iceland.jpg/330px-Norsemen_Landing_in_Iceland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Norsemen_Landing_in_Iceland.jpg/440px-Norsemen_Landing_in_Iceland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2768" data-file-height="1887" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Norsemen_Landing_in_Iceland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Norsemen landing in Iceland – a 19th-century depiction by <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wergeland" title="Oscar Wergeland">Oscar Wergeland</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_Iceland" title="Settlement of Iceland">Settlement of Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Commonwealth" title="Icelandic Commonwealth">Icelandic Commonwealth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christianisation_of_Iceland" class="mw-redirect" title="Christianisation of Iceland">Christianisation of Iceland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ingolf_by_Raadsig.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ingolf_by_Raadsig.jpg/220px-Ingolf_by_Raadsig.jpg" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ingolf_by_Raadsig.jpg/330px-Ingolf_by_Raadsig.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ingolf_by_Raadsig.jpg/440px-Ingolf_by_Raadsig.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1568" data-file-height="1244" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ingolf_by_Raadsig.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ing%C3%B3lfr_Arnarson" title="Ingólfr Arnarson">Ingólfr Arnarson</a> (modern Icelandic: <i>Ingólfur Arnarson</i>), the first permanent Scandinavian settler in Iceland</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to both <a href="/wiki/Landn%C3%A1mab%C3%B3k" title="Landnámabók">Landnámabók</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Dslendingab%C3%B3k" title="Íslendingabók">Íslendingabók</a>, Celtic monks known as the <a href="/wiki/Papar" title="Papar">Papar</a> lived in Iceland before Scandinavian settlers arrived, possibly members of a <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-Scottish_mission" title="Hiberno-Scottish mission">Hiberno-Scottish mission</a>. Recent <a href="/wiki/Archaeological" class="mw-redirect" title="Archaeological">archaeological</a> excavations have revealed the ruins of a cabin in <a href="/wiki/Hafnir" title="Hafnir">Hafnir</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Reykjanes" title="Reykjanes">Reykjanes peninsula</a>. <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dating" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon dating">Carbon dating</a> indicates that it was abandoned sometime between 770 and 880.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> In 2016, archeologists uncovered a <a href="/wiki/Longhouse" title="Longhouse">longhouse</a> in <a href="/wiki/St%C3%B6%C3%B0varfj%C3%B6r%C3%B0ur" title="Stöðvarfjörður">Stöðvarfjörður</a> that has been dated to as early as the year 800.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Swedish Viking explorer <a href="/wiki/Gar%C3%B0ar_Svavarsson" title="Garðar Svavarsson">Garðar Svavarsson</a> was the first to circumnavigate Iceland in 870 and establish that it was an island.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> He stayed over winter and built a house in <a href="/wiki/H%C3%BAsav%C3%ADk" title="Húsavík">Húsavík</a>. Garðar departed the following summer but one of his men, <a href="/wiki/N%C3%A1ttfari" title="Náttfari">Náttfari</a>, decided to stay behind with two slaves. Náttfari settled in what is now known as Náttfaravík and he and his slaves became the first permanent residents of Iceland.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Norwegian-Norse chieftain <a href="/wiki/Ing%C3%B3lfr_Arnarson" title="Ingólfr Arnarson">Ingólfr Arnarson</a> built his homestead in present-day <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk" title="Reykjavík">Reykjavík</a> in the year 874. Ingólfr was followed by many other emigrant settlers, largely Scandinavians and their <a href="/wiki/Thrall" title="Thrall">thralls</a>, many of whom were Irish or Scottish.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> By 930, most <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable land</a> on the island had been claimed; the <a href="/wiki/Althing" title="Althing">Althing</a>, a legislative and judicial assembly, was initiated to regulate the Icelandic Commonwealth. Lack of arable land also served impetus to the <a href="/wiki/Norse_colonization_of_Greenland" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse colonization of Greenland">settlement of Greenland</a> starting in 986.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The period of these early settlements coincided with the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">Medieval Warm Period</a>, when temperatures were similar to those of the early 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> At this time, about 25% of Iceland was covered with forest, compared to 1% in the present day.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Christianity was adopted <a href="/wiki/Christianisation_of_Iceland" class="mw-redirect" title="Christianisation of Iceland">by consensus</a> around 999–1000, although <a href="/wiki/Norse_paganism" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse paganism">Norse paganism</a> persisted among some segments of the population for some years afterwards.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Middle_Ages">The Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: The Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bolungarvik_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Bolungarvik_02.jpg/220px-Bolungarvik_02.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Bolungarvik_02.jpg/330px-Bolungarvik_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Bolungarvik_02.jpg/440px-Bolungarvik_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bolungarvik_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Ósvör</i>, a replica of an old fishing outpost outside <a href="/wiki/Bolungarv%C3%ADk" title="Bolungarvík">Bolungarvík</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Law_speaker.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Law_speaker.jpg/220px-Law_speaker.jpg" width="220" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Law_speaker.jpg/330px-Law_speaker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/44/Law_speaker.jpg 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="452" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Law_speaker.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 19th-century depiction of the <a href="/wiki/Al%C3%BEingi" class="mw-redirect" title="Alþingi">Alþingi</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Commonwealth" title="Icelandic Commonwealth">Commonwealth</a> in session at <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Eingvellir" title="Þingvellir">Þingvellir</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Sturlungs" title="Age of the Sturlungs">Age of the Sturlungs</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Commonwealth" title="Icelandic Commonwealth">Icelandic Commonwealth</a> lasted until the 13th century, when the political system devised by the original settlers proved unable to cope with the increasing power of Icelandic chieftains.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The internal struggles and civil strife of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Sturlungs" title="Age of the Sturlungs">Age of the Sturlungs</a> led to the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Covenant_(Iceland)" title="Old Covenant (Iceland)">Old Covenant</a> in 1262, which ended the Commonwealth and brought Iceland under the Norwegian crown. Possession of Iceland passed from the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian Empire">Norwegian Empire</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Kalmar_Union" title="Kalmar Union">Kalmar Union</a> in 1415, when the kingdoms of Norway, Denmark and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> were united. After the break-up of the union in 1523, it remained a Norwegian dependency, as a part of <a href="/wiki/Denmark%E2%80%93Norway" title="Denmark–Norway">Denmark–Norway</a>.</p>
<p>In the ensuing centuries, Iceland became one of the poorest countries in Europe. Infertile soil, volcanic eruptions, deforestation and an unforgiving climate made for harsh life in a society where subsistence depended almost entirely on agriculture. The <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> swept Iceland twice, first in 1402–1404 and again in 1494–1495.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The former outbreak killed 50% to 60% of the population, and the latter 30% to 50%.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reformation_and_the_Early_Modern_period">Reformation and the Early Modern period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Reformation and the Early Modern period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Reformation" title="Icelandic Reformation">Icelandic Reformation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danish-Icelandic_Trade_Monopoly" class="mw-redirect" title="Danish-Icelandic Trade Monopoly">Danish-Icelandic Trade Monopoly</a>, and <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B3%C3%B0uhar%C3%B0indin" class="mw-redirect" title="Móðuharðindin">Móðuharðindin</a></div>
<p>Around the middle of the 16th century, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, King <a href="/wiki/Christian_III_of_Denmark" title="Christian III of Denmark">Christian III of Denmark</a> began to impose <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a> on all his subjects. <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3n_Arason" title="Jón Arason">Jón Arason</a>, the last Catholic bishop of <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B3lar" title="Hólar">Hólar</a>, was beheaded in 1550 along with two of his sons. The country subsequently became officially Lutheran and Lutheranism has since remained the dominant religion.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gerardi_Mercatoris_Atlas,_sive,_Cosmographicae_Meditationes_de_Fabrica_Mundi_et_Fabricati_Figura_(24696368309).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Gerardi_Mercatoris_Atlas%2C_sive%2C_Cosmographicae_Meditationes_de_Fabrica_Mundi_et_Fabricati_Figura_%2824696368309%29.jpg/220px-Gerardi_Mercatoris_Atlas%2C_sive%2C_Cosmographicae_Meditationes_de_Fabrica_Mundi_et_Fabricati_Figura_%2824696368309%29.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Gerardi_Mercatoris_Atlas%2C_sive%2C_Cosmographicae_Meditationes_de_Fabrica_Mundi_et_Fabricati_Figura_%2824696368309%29.jpg/330px-Gerardi_Mercatoris_Atlas%2C_sive%2C_Cosmographicae_Meditationes_de_Fabrica_Mundi_et_Fabricati_Figura_%2824696368309%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Gerardi_Mercatoris_Atlas%2C_sive%2C_Cosmographicae_Meditationes_de_Fabrica_Mundi_et_Fabricati_Figura_%2824696368309%29.jpg/440px-Gerardi_Mercatoris_Atlas%2C_sive%2C_Cosmographicae_Meditationes_de_Fabrica_Mundi_et_Fabricati_Figura_%2824696368309%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1019" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gerardi_Mercatoris_Atlas,_sive,_Cosmographicae_Meditationes_de_Fabrica_Mundi_et_Fabricati_Figura_(24696368309).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A map of Iceland published in the early 17th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 17th and 18th centuries, Denmark imposed <a href="/wiki/Danish%E2%80%93Icelandic_Trade_Monopoly" title="Danish–Icelandic Trade Monopoly">harsh trade restrictions</a> on Iceland. Natural disasters, including volcanic eruption and disease, contributed to a decreasing population. Pirates from several countries, including the Barbary Coast, raided its coastal settlements and abducted people into slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> A great <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> epidemic in the 18th century killed around a third of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> In 1783 the <a href="/wiki/Laki" title="Laki">Laki</a> volcano erupted, with devastating effects.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> In the years following the eruption, known as the <a href="/wiki/Mist_Hardships" class="mw-redirect" title="Mist Hardships">Mist Hardships</a> (Icelandic: <i>Móðuharðindin</i>), over half of all livestock died in the country. Around a quarter of the population died in the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_movement_1814.E2.80.931918">Independence movement 1814–1918</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Independence movement 1814–1918">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_independence_movement" title="Icelandic independence movement">Icelandic independence movement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fj%C3%B6lnir_(journal)" title="Fjölnir (journal)">Fjölnir (journal)</a></div>
<p>In 1814, following the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, Denmark-Norway was broken up into two separate kingdoms via the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Kiel" title="Treaty of Kiel">Treaty of Kiel</a> but Iceland remained a Danish dependency. Throughout the 19th century, the country's climate continued to grow colder, resulting in mass emigration to the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>, particularly to the region of <a href="/wiki/Gimli,_Manitoba" title="Gimli, Manitoba">Gimli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">Manitoba</a> in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, which was sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/New_Iceland" title="New Iceland">New Iceland</a>. About 15,000 people emigrated, out of a total population of 70,000.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>A national consciousness arose in the first half of the 19th century, inspired by <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">romantic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalist</a> ideas from mainland <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. An Icelandic independence movement took shape in the 1850s under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3n_Sigur%C3%B0sson" title="Jón Sigurðsson">Jón Sigurðsson</a>, based on the burgeoning Icelandic nationalism inspired by the <i><a href="/wiki/Fj%C3%B6lnismenn" class="mw-redirect" title="Fjölnismenn">Fjölnismenn</a></i> and other Danish-educated Icelandic intellectuals. In 1874, Denmark granted Iceland a constitution and limited home rule. This was expanded in 1904, and <a href="/wiki/Hannes_Hafstein" title="Hannes Hafstein">Hannes Hafstein</a> served as the first <a href="/wiki/Minister_for_Iceland" title="Minister for Iceland">Minister for Iceland</a> in the Danish cabinet.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Iceland_1918.E2.80.931944">Kingdom of Iceland 1918–1944</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Iceland 1918–1944">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iceland" title="Kingdom of Iceland">Kingdom of Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Iceland" title="Invasion of Iceland">Invasion of Iceland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iceland_in_World_War_II" title="Iceland in World War II">Iceland in World War II</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Berwick_(65).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/HMS_Berwick_%2865%29.jpg/220px-HMS_Berwick_%2865%29.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/HMS_Berwick_%2865%29.jpg/330px-HMS_Berwick_%2865%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/HMS_Berwick_%2865%29.jpg/440px-HMS_Berwick_%2865%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="784" data-file-height="598" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Berwick_(65).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/HMS_Berwick_(65)" title="HMS Berwick (65)">HMS&#160;<i>Berwick</i></a> led the British <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Iceland" title="Invasion of Iceland">invasion of Iceland</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Danish%E2%80%93Icelandic_Act_of_Union" title="Danish–Icelandic Act of Union">Danish–Icelandic Act of Union</a>, an agreement with Denmark signed on 1 December 1918 and valid for 25&#160;years, recognised Iceland as a fully sovereign state in a <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> with Denmark. The Government of Iceland established an embassy in Copenhagen and requested that Denmark handle Icelandic foreign policy. Danish embassies around the world displayed two coats of arms and two flags: those of the Kingdom of Denmark and those of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iceland" title="Kingdom of Iceland">Kingdom of Iceland</a>.</p>
<p>During World War II, Iceland joined Denmark in asserting neutrality. After the <a href="/wiki/German_occupation_of_Denmark" class="mw-redirect" title="German occupation of Denmark">German occupation of Denmark</a> on 9 April 1940, the Althing replaced the King with a regent and declared that the Icelandic government would take control of foreign affairs and other matters previously handled by Denmark. A month later, British armed forces <a href="/wiki/Operation_Fork" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Fork">invaded and occupied the country</a>, violating Icelandic <a href="/wiki/Neutrality_(international_relations)" class="mw-redirect" title="Neutrality (international relations)">neutrality</a>. In 1941, the occupation was taken over by the United States so that Britain could use its troops elsewhere.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independent_republic_1944.E2.80.93present">Independent republic 1944–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Independent republic 1944–present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scylla-Odinn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Scylla-Odinn.jpg/220px-Scylla-Odinn.jpg" width="220" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Scylla-Odinn.jpg/330px-Scylla-Odinn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Scylla-Odinn.jpg/440px-Scylla-Odinn.jpg 2x" data-file-width="902" data-file-height="547" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scylla-Odinn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
British and Icelandic vessels collide in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Cod_Wars" title="Cod Wars">Cod Wars</a> (Icelandic vessel is shown on the left; the British vessel is on the right)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Founding_of_the_Republic_of_Iceland" title="Founding of the Republic of Iceland">Founding of the Republic of Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland_in_the_Cold_War" title="Iceland in the Cold War">Iceland in the Cold War</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cod_Wars" title="Cod Wars">Cod Wars</a></div>
<p>On 31 December 1943, the <a href="/wiki/Danish%E2%80%93Icelandic_Act_of_Union" title="Danish–Icelandic Act of Union">Danish–Icelandic Act of Union</a> expired after 25&#160;years. Beginning on 20 May 1944, Icelanders voted in a four-day plebiscite on whether to terminate the personal union with Denmark, abolish the monarchy, and establish a republic. The vote was 97% to end the union, and 95% in favour of the new republican constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Iceland formally became a republic on 17 June 1944, with <a href="/wiki/Sveinn_Bj%C3%B6rnsson" title="Sveinn Björnsson">Sveinn Björnsson</a> as its first president.</p>
<p>In 1946, the Allied occupation force left Iceland. The nation formally became a member of <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> on 30 March 1949, amid <a href="/wiki/1949_anti-NATO_riot_in_Iceland" title="1949 anti-NATO riot in Iceland">domestic controversy and riots</a>. On 5 May 1951, a defence agreement was signed with the United States. American troops returned to Iceland as the <a href="/wiki/Iceland_Defence_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="Iceland Defence Force">Iceland Defence Force</a>, and remained throughout the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. The US withdrew the last of its forces on 30 September 2006.</p>
<p>Iceland had prospered during the war. The immediate post-war period was followed by substantial <a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">economic growth</a>, driven by industrialisation of the fishing industry and the US <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> programme, through which Icelanders received the most aid per capita of any European country (at USD 209, with the war-ravaged <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> a distant second at USD 109).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 1970s were marked by the <a href="/wiki/Cod_Wars" title="Cod Wars">Cod Wars</a>&#160;— several disputes with the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> over Iceland's extension of its fishing limits to 200&#160;miles offshore. Iceland hosted a <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk_Summit" title="Reykjavík Summit">summit in Reykjavík in 1986</a> between United States President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> and Soviet Premier <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a>, during which they took significant steps toward <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_disarmament" title="Nuclear disarmament">nuclear disarmament</a>. A few years later, Iceland became the first country to recognize the independence of <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> as they <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">broke away</a> from the USSR. Throughout the 1990s, the country expanded its international role and developed a foreign policy oriented toward humanitarian and peacekeeping causes. To that end, Iceland provided aid and expertise to various NATO-led interventions in <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_War" title="Bosnian War">Bosnia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kosovo_War" title="Kosovo War">Kosovo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/First_Gulf_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First Gulf War">Iraq</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Iceland joined the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a> in 1994, after which the economy was greatly diversified and liberalised. International economic relations increased further after 2001, when Iceland's newly deregulated banks began to raise massive amounts of <a href="/wiki/External_debt" title="External debt">external debt</a>, contributing to a 32% increase in Iceland's <a href="/wiki/Gross_national_income" title="Gross national income">gross national income</a> between 2002 and 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-jackson_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jackson-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_boom_and_crisis">Economic boom and crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Economic boom and crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932011_Icelandic_financial_crisis" title="2008–2011 Icelandic financial crisis">2008–2011 Icelandic financial crisis</a> and <a href="/wiki/2009_Icelandic_financial_crisis_protests" title="2009 Icelandic financial crisis protests">2009 Icelandic financial crisis protests</a></div>
<p>In the years 2003–2007, following the privatization of the banking sector under the government of <a href="/wiki/Dav%C3%AD%C3%B0_Oddsson" title="Davíð Oddsson">Davíð Oddsson</a>, Iceland moved toward having an economy based on international investment banking and financial services.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-42">[42]</a></sup> It was quickly becoming one of the most prosperous countries in the world but was hit hard by a <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932011_Icelandic_financial_crisis" title="2008–2011 Icelandic financial crisis">major financial crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-42">[42]</a></sup> The crisis resulted in the greatest migration from Iceland since 1887, with a net emigration of 5,000 people in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-nordregio.se_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nordregio.se-43">[43]</a></sup> Iceland's economy stabilised under the government of <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3hanna_Sigur%C3%B0ard%C3%B3ttir" title="Jóhanna Sigurðardóttir">Jóhanna Sigurðardóttir</a>, and grew by 1.6% in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Many Icelanders, however, have remained unhappy with the state of the economy and government austerity policies. The centre-right <a href="/wiki/Independence_Party_(Iceland)" title="Independence Party (Iceland)">Independence Party</a> was returned to power in coalition with the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Party_(Iceland)" title="Progressive Party (Iceland)">Progressive Party</a> in the 2013 elections.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Iceland.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Map_of_Iceland.svg/220px-Map_of_Iceland.svg.png" width="220" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Map_of_Iceland.svg/330px-Map_of_Iceland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Map_of_Iceland.svg/440px-Map_of_Iceland.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1834" data-file-height="1489" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Iceland.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
General topographic map</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Iceland" title="Geography of Iceland">Geography of Iceland</a>.</div>
<p>Iceland is at the juncture of the North Atlantic and Arctic Oceans. The main island is entirely south of the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a>, which passes through the small Icelandic island of <a href="/wiki/Gr%C3%ADmsey" title="Grímsey">Grímsey</a> off the main island's northern coast. The country lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/63rd_parallel_north" title="63rd parallel north">63</a> and <a href="/wiki/68th_parallel_north" title="68th parallel north">68°N</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/25th_meridian_west" title="25th meridian west">25</a> and <a href="/wiki/13th_meridian_west" title="13th meridian west">13°W</a>.</p>
<p>Iceland is closer to <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">continental Europe</a> than to mainland North America; thus, the island is generally included in Europe for historical, political, cultural, and practical reasons. Geologically, the island includes parts of both continental plates. The closest body of land is <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> (290&#160;km, 180&#160;mi). The closest bodies of land in Europe are the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a> (420&#160;km, 260&#160;mi); <a href="/wiki/Jan_Mayen" title="Jan Mayen">Jan Mayen</a> Island (570&#160;km, 350&#160;mi); <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">Outer Hebrides</a>, both about 740&#160;km (460&#160;mi); and the Scottish mainland and <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a>, both about 750&#160;km (470&#160;mi). The mainland of Norway is about 970&#160;km (600&#160;mi) away.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Icelandic_landscapes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Icelandic_landscapes.jpg/220px-Icelandic_landscapes.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Icelandic_landscapes.jpg/330px-Icelandic_landscapes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Icelandic_landscapes.jpg/440px-Icelandic_landscapes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8192" data-file-height="5568" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Icelandic_landscapes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A sample of three typical Icelandic landscapes</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Iceland is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_by_area" title="List of islands by area">world's 18th largest island</a>, and Europe's second-largest island after Great Britain. The main island is 101,826&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39,315&#160;sq&#160;mi), but the entire country is 103,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39,768.5&#160;sq&#160;mi) in size, of which 62.7% is <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a>. About 30 minor islands are in Iceland, including the lightly populated Grímsey and the <a href="/wiki/Vestmannaeyjar" title="Vestmannaeyjar">Vestmannaeyjar</a> archipelago. Lakes and glaciers cover 14.3% of its surface; only 23% is vegetated.<sup id="cite_ref-NLS_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NLS-46">[46]</a></sup> The largest lakes are <a href="/wiki/%C3%9E%C3%B3risvatn" title="Þórisvatn">Þórisvatn</a> reservoir: 83–88&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (32–34&#160;sq&#160;mi) and <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Eingvallavatn" title="Þingvallavatn">Þingvallavatn</a>: 82&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (32&#160;sq&#160;mi); other important lakes include <a href="/wiki/Lagarflj%C3%B3t" title="Lagarfljót">Lagarfljót</a> and <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BDvatn" title="Mývatn">Mývatn</a>. <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6kuls%C3%A1rl%C3%B3n" title="Jökulsárlón">Jökulsárlón</a> is the deepest lake, at 248&#160;m (814&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Geologically, Iceland is part of the <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_Ridge" title="Mid-Atlantic Ridge">Mid-Atlantic Ridge</a>, a ridge along which the <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_crust" title="Oceanic crust">oceanic crust</a> spreads and forms new oceanic crust. This part of the mid-ocean ridge is located above a mantle plume, causing Iceland to be <a href="/wiki/Subaerial" title="Subaerial">subaerial</a> (above the surface of the sea). The ridge marks the boundary between the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_American_Plate" title="North American Plate">North American Plates</a>, and Iceland was created by <a href="/wiki/Rift" title="Rift">rifting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(geology)" title="Accretion (geology)">accretion</a> through volcanism along the ridge.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many <a href="/wiki/Fjord" title="Fjord">fjords</a> punctuate Iceland's 4,970-km-long (3,088-mi) coastline, which is also where most settlements are situated. The island's interior, the <a href="/wiki/Highlands_of_Iceland" title="Highlands of Iceland">Highlands of Iceland</a>, is a cold and uninhabitable combination of sand, mountains, and <a href="/wiki/Lava_field" title="Lava field">lava fields</a>. The major towns are the capital city of Reykjavík, along with its outlying towns of <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B3pavogur" title="Kópavogur">Kópavogur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hafnarfj%C3%B6r%C3%B0ur" title="Hafnarfjörður">Hafnarfjörður</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gar%C3%B0ab%C3%A6r" title="Garðabær">Garðabær</a>, nearby <a href="/wiki/Reykjanesb%C3%A6r" title="Reykjanesbær">Reykjanesbær</a> where the international airport is located, and the town of <a href="/wiki/Akureyri" title="Akureyri">Akureyri</a> in northern Iceland. The island of Grímsey on the Arctic Circle contains the northernmost habitation of Iceland, whereas <a href="/wiki/Kolbeinsey" title="Kolbeinsey">Kolbeinsey</a> contains the northernmost point of Iceland.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Govt_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Govt-49">[49]</a></sup> Iceland has <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_parks_of_Iceland" title="List of national parks of Iceland">three national parks</a>: <a href="/wiki/Vatnaj%C3%B6kull_National_Park" title="Vatnajökull National Park">Vatnajökull National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sn%C3%A6fellsj%C3%B6kull" title="Snæfellsjökull">Snæfellsjökull National Park</a>, and <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Eingvellir" title="Þingvellir">Þingvellir National Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The country is considered a "strong performer" in environmental protection, having been ranked 13th in <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a>'s Environmental Performance Index of 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<center>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Iceland" title="Geology of Iceland">Geology of Iceland</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Iceland_plume" title="Iceland plume">Iceland plume</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Erupting_geysir.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Erupting_geysir.jpg/220px-Erupting_geysir.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Erupting_geysir.jpg/330px-Erupting_geysir.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Erupting_geysir.jpg/440px-Erupting_geysir.jpg 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="333" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Erupting_geysir.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The erupting <a href="/wiki/Geysir" title="Geysir">Geysir</a> in <a href="/wiki/Haukadalur" title="Haukadalur">Haukadalur</a> valley, the oldest known <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">geyser</a> in the world</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gullfoss,_an_iconic_waterfall_of_Iceland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Gullfoss%2C_an_iconic_waterfall_of_Iceland.jpg/220px-Gullfoss%2C_an_iconic_waterfall_of_Iceland.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Gullfoss%2C_an_iconic_waterfall_of_Iceland.jpg/330px-Gullfoss%2C_an_iconic_waterfall_of_Iceland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Gullfoss%2C_an_iconic_waterfall_of_Iceland.jpg/440px-Gullfoss%2C_an_iconic_waterfall_of_Iceland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gullfoss,_an_iconic_waterfall_of_Iceland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Gullfoss" title="Gullfoss">Gullfoss</a>, an iconic waterfall of Iceland</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A geologically young land, Iceland is located on both the <a href="/wiki/Iceland_hotspot" title="Iceland hotspot">Iceland hotspot</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_Ridge" title="Mid-Atlantic Ridge">Mid-Atlantic Ridge</a>, which runs right through it. This location means that the island is highly geologically active with many volcanoes, notably <a href="/wiki/Hekla" title="Hekla">Hekla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eldgj%C3%A1" title="Eldgjá">Eldgjá</a>, <a href="/wiki/Her%C3%B0ubrei%C3%B0" title="Herðubreið">Herðubreið</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eldfell" title="Eldfell">Eldfell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The volcanic eruption of <a href="/wiki/Laki" title="Laki">Laki</a> in 1783–1784 caused a famine that killed nearly a quarter of the island's population.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> In addition, the eruption caused dust clouds and haze to appear over most of Europe and parts of Asia and Africa for several months afterward, and affected climates in other areas.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Iceland has many <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">geysers</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Geysir" title="Geysir">Geysir</a>, from which the English word is derived, and the famous <a href="/wiki/Strokkur" title="Strokkur">Strokkur</a>, which erupts every 8–10&#160;minutes. After a phase of inactivity, Geysir started erupting again after a <a href="/wiki/2000_Iceland_earthquakes" title="2000 Iceland earthquakes">series of earthquakes in 2000</a>. Geysir has since grown quieter and does not erupt often.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the widespread availability of <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">geothermal power</a>, and the harnessing of many rivers and waterfalls for <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a>, most residents have access to inexpensive hot water, heating, and electricity. The island is composed primarily of <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a>, a low-<a href="/wiki/Silica" class="mw-redirect" title="Silica">silica</a> lava associated with <a href="/wiki/Effusive_eruption" title="Effusive eruption">effusive volcanism</a> as has occurred also in <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>. Iceland, however, has a variety of volcanic types (composite and fissure), many producing more evolved lavas such as <a href="/wiki/Rhyolite" title="Rhyolite">rhyolite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andesite" title="Andesite">andesite</a>. Iceland has hundreds of volcanoes with about 30 active volcanic systems.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Surtsey" title="Surtsey">Surtsey</a>, one of the youngest islands in the world, is part of Iceland. Named after <a href="/wiki/Surtr" title="Surtr">Surtr</a>, it rose above the ocean in a series of volcanic eruptions between 8 November 1963 and 5 June 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Govt_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Govt-49">[49]</a></sup> Only scientists researching the growth of new life are allowed to visit the island.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 21 March 2010, a <a href="/wiki/2010_eruptions_of_Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull" title="2010 eruptions of Eyjafjallajökull">volcano in Eyjafjallajökull</a> in the south of Iceland erupted for the first time since 1821, forcing 600 people to flee their homes.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Additional eruptions on 14 April forced hundreds of people to abandon their homes.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The resultant cloud of <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_ash" title="Volcanic ash">volcanic ash</a> brought <a href="/wiki/Air_travel_disruption_after_the_2010_Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull_eruption" title="Air travel disruption after the 2010 Eyjafjallajökull eruption">major disruption to air travel</a> across Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another large eruption occurred on 21 May 2011. This time it was the <a href="/wiki/Gr%C3%ADmsv%C3%B6tn" title="Grímsvötn">Grímsvötn</a> volcano, located under the thick ice of Europe's largest glacier, <a href="/wiki/Vatnaj%C3%B6kull" title="Vatnajökull">Vatnajökull</a>. Grímsvötn is one of Iceland's most active volcanoes, and this eruption was much more powerful than the 2010 Eyjafjallajökull activity, with ash and lava hurled 20&#160;km (12&#160;mi) into the atmosphere, creating a large cloud.<sup id="cite_ref-connor_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-connor-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>The highest elevation for Iceland is listed as 2,110 m (6,923&#160;ft) at Hvannadalshnúkur (64°00′N 16°39′W).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iceland_koppen.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Iceland_koppen.svg/220px-Iceland_koppen.svg.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Iceland_koppen.svg/330px-Iceland_koppen.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Iceland_koppen.svg/440px-Iceland_koppen.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="765" data-file-height="765" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iceland_koppen.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a> types of Iceland</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull.jpeg/220px-Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull.jpeg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull.jpeg/330px-Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull.jpeg/440px-Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="2960" data-file-height="1983" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull" title="Eyjafjallajökull">Eyjafjallajökull</a> glacier, one of the smaller <a href="/wiki/Glaciers_of_Iceland" class="mw-redirect" title="Glaciers of Iceland">glaciers of Iceland</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Iceland" title="Climate of Iceland">Climate of Iceland</a></div>
<p>The climate of Iceland's coast is <a href="/wiki/Subarctic_climate" title="Subarctic climate">subarctic</a>. The warm <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Current" title="North Atlantic Current">North Atlantic Current</a> ensures generally higher annual temperatures than in most places of similar latitude in the world. Regions in the world with similar climates include the <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_Islands" title="Aleutian Islands">Aleutian Islands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Peninsula" title="Alaska Peninsula">Alaska Peninsula</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a>, although these regions are closer to the equator. Despite its proximity to the Arctic, the island's coasts remain ice-free through the winter. Ice incursions are rare, the last having occurred on the north coast in 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>The climate varies between different parts of the island. Generally speaking, the south coast is warmer, wetter, and windier than the north. The Central Highlands are the coldest part of the country. Low-lying inland areas in the north are the most arid. Snowfall in winter is more common in the north than the south.</p>
<p>The highest air temperature recorded was 30.5&#160;°C (86.9&#160;°F) on 22 June 1939 at Teigarhorn on the southeastern coast. The lowest was −38&#160;°C (−36.4&#160;°F) on 22 January 1918 at Grímsstaðir and Möðrudalur in the northeastern hinterland. The temperature records for Reykjavík are 26.2&#160;°C (79.2&#160;°F) on 30 July 2008, and −24.5&#160;°C (−12.1&#160;°F) on 21 January 1918.</p>
