</div>
<p>In December 2007, 33,678 people (13.5% of the total population) living in Iceland had been born abroad, including children of Icelandic parents living abroad. Around 19,000 people (6% of the population) held foreign citizenship. <a href="/wiki/Polish_diaspora" title="Polish diaspora">Polish</a> people make up the largest minority group by a considerable margin, and still form the bulk of the foreign workforce. About 8,000 Poles now live in Iceland, 1,500 of them in <a href="/wiki/Fjar%C3%B0abygg%C3%B0" title="Fjarðabyggð">Fjarðabyggð</a> where they make up 75% of the workforce who are constructing the Fjarðarál aluminium plant.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> The recent increase in <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a> has been credited<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (November 2014)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> to a <a href="/wiki/Labour_shortage" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour shortage">labour shortage</a> due to the booming economy at the time, as well as to the lifting of restrictions on the movement of people from the countries that were a part of the <a href="/wiki/2004_enlargement_of_the_European_Union" title="2004 enlargement of the European Union">2004 enlargement of the European Union</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Large-scale construction projects in the east of Iceland (see <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1rahnj%C3%BAkar_Hydropower_Plant" title="Kárahnjúkar Hydropower Plant">Kárahnjúkar Hydropower Plant</a>) have also brought in many people whose stay is expected to be temporary. Many Polish immigrants were also considering leaving in 2008 as a result of the <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932011_Icelandic_financial_crisis" title="2008–2011 Icelandic financial crisis">Icelandic financial crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>The southwest corner of Iceland is the most densely populated region. It is also the location of the capital Reykjavík, the northernmost national capital in the world. The largest towns outside the Greater Reykjavík area are <a href="/wiki/Akureyri" title="Akureyri">Akureyri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reykjanesb%C3%A6r" title="Reykjanesbær">Reykjanesbær</a>, although the latter is relatively close to the capital.</p>
<p>Some 500 Icelanders under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Erik_the_Red" title="Erik the Red">Erik the Red</a> colonised <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> among the existing <a href="/wiki/Greenland#Early_Paleo-Eskimo_cultures" title="Greenland">paleo-Eskimo inhabitants</a> in the late 10th century.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> The total population reached a high point of perhaps 5,000 and developed independent institutions before disappearing by 1500.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> People from Greenland attempted to set up a colony at <a href="/wiki/Vinland" title="Vinland">Vinland</a> in North America, but it was abandoned in the face of hostility from the indigenous residents.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> Emigration to the United States and Canada began in the 1870s. As of 2006<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Canada had over 88,000 people of <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Canadian" class="mw-redirect" title="Icelandic Canadian">Icelandic</a> descent,<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> while there are more than 40,000 Americans of <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Icelandic American">Icelandic</a> descent, according to the 2000 US census.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Iceland" title="Languages of Iceland">Languages of Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic language</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_name" title="Icelandic name">Icelandic name</a></div>
<p>Iceland's official written and spoken language is <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>, a <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">North Germanic language</a> descended from <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a>. In grammar and vocabulary, it has changed less from Old Norse than the other Nordic languages; Icelandic has preserved more verb and noun <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflection</a>, and has to a considerable extent developed new vocabulary based on native roots rather than borrowings from other languages. The puristic tendency in the development of Icelandic vocabulary is to a large degree a result of conscious language planning, in addition to centuries of isolation. Icelandic is the only living language to retain the use of the <a href="/wiki/Runic" class="mw-redirect" title="Runic">runic</a> letter <a href="/wiki/%C3%9E" class="mw-redirect" title="Þ">Þ</a> in <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a>. The closest living relative of the Icelandic language is <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Sign_Language" title="Icelandic Sign Language">Icelandic Sign Language</a> was officially recognised as a minority language in 2011. In education, its use for Iceland's deaf community is <a href="/wiki/Legal_recognition_of_sign_languages" title="Legal recognition of sign languages">regulated</a> by the <i>National Curriculum Guide</i>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> and <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a> are compulsory subjects in the school curriculum. Both languages are widely understood and spoken.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> Other commonly spoken languages are <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>. <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a> is mostly spoken by the local Polish community (the biggest minority of Iceland), and Danish is mostly spoken in a way largely comprehensible to Swedes and Norwegians—it is often referred to as <i>skandinavíska</i> (i. e. <i>Scandinavian</i>) in Iceland.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rather than using family names, as is the usual custom in most western nations, Icelanders carry <a href="/wiki/Patronymic" title="Patronymic">patronymic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Matronymic" title="Matronymic">matronymic</a> surnames, patronyms being far more commonly practiced. Patronymic last names are based on the first name of the father, while matronymic names are based on the first name of the mother. These follow the person's given name, e.g. <i>Elísabet Jónsdóttir</i> ("Elísabet, Jón's daughter" (Jón, being the father)) or <i>Ólafur Katrínarson</i> ("Ólafur, Katrín's son" (Katrín being the mother)).<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> Consequently, Icelanders refer to one another by their given name, and the Icelandic telephone directory is listed alphabetically by first name rather than by surname.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> All new names must be decided upon by the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Naming_Committee" title="Icelandic Naming Committee">Icelandic Naming Committee</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Iceland has a <a href="/wiki/Universal_health_care" title="Universal health care">universal health care</a> system that is administered by its Ministry of Welfare (<a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>: <span lang="is"><i>Velferðarráðuneytið</i></span>)<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> and paid for mostly by taxes (85%) and to a lesser extent by service fees (15%). Unlike most countries, there are no private hospitals, and private insurance is practically nonexistent.<sup id="cite_ref-j1_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-j1-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>A considerable portion of the government budget is assigned to health care,<sup id="cite_ref-j1_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-j1-173">[173]</a></sup> and Iceland ranks 11th in health care expenditures as a percentage of GDP<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> and 14th in spending per capita.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> Over all, the country's health care system is one of the best performing in the world, ranked 15th by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> According to an OECD report, Iceland devotes far more resources to healthcare than most industrialised nations. As of 2009<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Iceland had 3.7 doctors per 1,000 people (compared with an average of 3.1 in OECD countries) and 15.3 nurses per 1,000 people (compared with an OECD average of 8.4).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>Icelanders are among the world's healthiest people, with 81% reporting they are in good health, according to an OECD survey.<sup id="cite_ref-Iceland_.E2.80.93_OECD_Better_Life_Index_82-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iceland_.E2.80.93_OECD_Better_Life_Index-82">[82]</a></sup> Although it is a growing problem, <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a> is not as prevalent as in other <a href="/wiki/Developed_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Developed countries">developed countries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-177">[177]</a></sup> Iceland has many campaigns for health and wellbeing. Most famously, the television show <a href="/wiki/LazyTown" title="LazyTown">Lazytown</a>, starring and created by former gymnastics champion <a href="/wiki/Magn%C3%BAs_Scheving" title="Magnús Scheving">Magnus Scheving</a>. <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">Infant mortality</a> is one of the lowest in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> and the proportion of the population that smokes is lower than the OECD average.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_177-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-177">[177]</a></sup> Almost all women choose to terminate pregnancies of children with <a href="/wiki/Down_Syndrome" class="mw-redirect" title="Down Syndrome">Down Syndrome</a> in Iceland.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> The average <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> is 81.8 (compared to an OECD average of 79.5), the 4th highest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>Additionally, Iceland has a very low level of pollution, thanks to an overwhelming reliance on cleaner geothermal energy, a low population density, and a high level of environmental consciousness among <a href="/wiki/Citizens" class="mw-redirect" title="Citizens">citizens</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> According to an OECD assessment, the amount of toxic material in the atmosphere is far lower than any other industrialised country measured.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;"><a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a></td>

<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;"><a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a></td>
<td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;">
<div style="background:Green; width:0.3%; overflow: hidden;">&#160; </div>
</td>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">0.3%</td>

<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;"><a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Ethical_Humanist_Association" title="Icelandic Ethical Humanist Association">Humanist association</a></td>
<td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;">
<div style="background:Yellow; width:0.4%; overflow: hidden;">&#160; </div>
</td>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">0.4%</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;"><a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bahá'í Faith">Bahá'í Faith</a></td>
<td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;">
<div style="background:Khaki; width:0.1%; overflow: hidden;">&#160; </div>
</td>
<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">0.1%</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
<p>Icelanders have freedom of religion guaranteed under the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Iceland" title="Constitution of Iceland">Constitution of Iceland</a>, although the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Iceland" title="Church of Iceland">Church of Iceland</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> body, is the <a href="/wiki/State_church" class="mw-redirect" title="State church">state church</a>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Evangelical Lutheran Church shall be the State Church in Iceland and, as such, it shall be supported and protected by the State.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Article 62, Section IV of <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Iceland" title="Constitution of Iceland">Constitution of Iceland</a><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The Registers Iceland keeps account of the religious affiliation of every Icelandic citizen. In 2015, Icelanders were divided into religious groups as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>71.6% members of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Iceland" title="Church of Iceland">Church of Iceland</a>.</li>
<li>11.6% members of some other <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> denomination.</li>
<li>9.0% other and not specified</li>
<li>5.8% unaffiliated</li>
<li>1.7% members of non-<a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> denomination.</li>
<li>0.4% members of the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Ethical_Humanist_Association" title="Icelandic Ethical Humanist Association">Icelandic Ethical Humanist Association</a></li>
</ul>
<p>Iceland is a very <a href="/wiki/Secular" class="mw-redirect" title="Secular">secular</a> country; as with other Nordic nations, religious attendance is relatively low.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> The above statistics represent administrative membership of religious organisations, which does not necessarily reflect the belief demographics of the population of Iceland. According to a study published in 2001, 23% of the inhabitants were either <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheist</a> or <a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">agnostic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> A Gallup poll conducted in 2012 found that 57% of Icelanders considered themselves "religious", 31% considered themselves "non-religious", while 10% defined themselves as "convinced atheists", placing Iceland among the ten countries with the highest proportions of atheists in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Religion-and-Atheism_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Religion-and-Atheism-188">[188]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Iceland" title="Culture of Iceland">Culture of Iceland</a></div>
<p>Icelandic culture has its roots in North Germanic traditions. <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_literature" title="Icelandic literature">Icelandic literature</a> is popular, in particular the <a href="/wiki/Sagas_of_Icelanders" title="Sagas of Icelanders">sagas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edda" title="Edda">eddas</a> that were written during the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High</a> and <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a>. Centuries of isolation have helped to insulate the country's Nordic culture from external influence; a prominent example is the preservation of the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic language</a>, which remains the closest to Old Norse of all modern Nordic languages.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In contrast to other Nordic countries, Icelanders place relatively great importance on independence and self-sufficiency; in a public opinion analysis conducted by the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>, over 85% of Icelanders believe independence is "very important," compared to 47% of Norwegians, 49% of Danes, and an average of 53% for the EU25.<sup id="cite_ref-europeancommission_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-europeancommission-189">[189]</a></sup> Icelanders also have a very strong work ethic, working some of the longest hours of any industrialised nation.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a poll conducted by the OECD, 66% of Icelanders were satisfied with their lives, while 70% believed that their lives will be satisfying in the future. Similarly, 83% of people in Iceland reported having more positive experiences in an average day than negative ones, compared to an OECD average of 72%, which makes Iceland one of the <a href="/wiki/Happiest_country" class="mw-redirect" title="Happiest country">happiest countries</a> in the OECD.<sup id="cite_ref-Iceland_.E2.80.93_OECD_Better_Life_Index_82-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iceland_.E2.80.93_OECD_Better_Life_Index-82">[82]</a></sup> A more recent 2012 survey found that around three quarters of respondents stated they were satisfied with their lives, compared to a global average of about 53%.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>Iceland is liberal with regard to <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">LGBT</a> rights issues. In 1996, the Icelandic parliament passed legislation to create <a href="/wiki/Registered_partnership" class="mw-redirect" title="Registered partnership">registered partnerships</a> for same-sex couples, conferring nearly all the rights and benefits of marriage. In 2006, parliament voted unanimously to grant same-sex couples the same rights as heterosexual couples in adoption, parenting and assisted insemination treatment. On 11 June 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Iceland" title="Same-sex marriage in Iceland">Icelandic parliament amended the marriage law</a>, making it gender neutral and defining marriage as between two individuals, making Iceland one of the first countries in the world to legalise <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a>. The law took effect on 27 June 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-gaymarriage_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gaymarriage-192">[192]</a></sup> The amendment to the law also means registered partnerships for same-sex couples are now no longer possible, and marriage is their only option—identical to the existing situation for opposite-sex couples.<sup id="cite_ref-gaymarriage_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gaymarriage-192">[192]</a></sup></p>
<p>Icelanders are known for their deep sense of community: an OECD survey found that 98% believe they know someone they could rely on in a time of need, higher than in any other industrialised country. Similarly, only 6% reported "rarely" or "never" socializing with others.<sup id="cite_ref-Iceland_.E2.80.93_OECD_Better_Life_Index_82-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iceland_.E2.80.93_OECD_Better_Life_Index-82">[82]</a></sup> This high level of social cohesion is attributed to the small size and homogeneity of the population, as well as to a long history of harsh survival in an isolated environment, which reinforced the importance of unity and cooperation.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Egalitarianism" title="Egalitarianism">Egalitarianism</a> is highly valued among the people of Iceland, with income inequality being among the lowest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-102">[102]</a></sup> The constitution explicitly prohibits the enactment of noble privileges, titles, and ranks.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated60_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated60-194">[194]</a></sup> Everyone is addressed by their first name. As in other Nordic countries, equality between the sexes is very high; Iceland is consistently ranked among the top three countries in the world for women to live in.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_literature" title="Icelandic literature">Icelandic literature</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:M%C3%B6%C3%B0ruvallab%C3%B3k_f13r.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/M%C3%B6%C3%B0ruvallab%C3%B3k_f13r.jpg/220px-M%C3%B6%C3%B0ruvallab%C3%B3k_f13r.jpg" width="220" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/M%C3%B6%C3%B0ruvallab%C3%B3k_f13r.jpg/330px-M%C3%B6%C3%B0ruvallab%C3%B3k_f13r.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/M%C3%B6%C3%B0ruvallab%C3%B3k_f13r.jpg/440px-M%C3%B6%C3%B0ruvallab%C3%B3k_f13r.jpg 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="491" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:M%C3%B6%C3%B0ruvallab%C3%B3k_f13r.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A page of <a href="/wiki/Nj%C3%A1ls_saga" title="Njáls saga">Njáls saga</a> from <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6%C3%B0ruvallab%C3%B3k" title="Möðruvallabók">Möðruvallabók</a>. The sagas are a significant part of the Icelandic heritage</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Iceland's best-known classical works of literature are the <a href="/wiki/Icelanders%27_sagas" class="mw-redirect" title="Icelanders' sagas">Icelanders' sagas</a>, prose epics set in Iceland's age of settlement. The most famous of these include <i><a href="/wiki/Nj%C3%A1ls_saga" title="Njáls saga">Njáls saga</a></i>, about an epic blood feud, and <i><a href="/wiki/Gr%C3%A6nlendinga_saga" class="mw-redirect" title="Grænlendinga saga">Grænlendinga saga</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Saga_of_Erik_the_Red" title="Saga of Erik the Red">Eiríks saga</a></i>, describing the discovery and settlement of Greenland and Vinland (modern <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland</a>). <i><a href="/wiki/Egils_saga" class="mw-redirect" title="Egils saga">Egils saga</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Laxd%C3%A6la_saga" title="Laxdæla saga">Laxdæla saga</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Grettis_saga" title="Grettis saga">Grettis saga</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/G%C3%ADsla_saga" title="Gísla saga">Gísla saga</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Gunnlaugs_saga_ormstungu" title="Gunnlaugs saga ormstungu">Gunnlaugs saga ormstungu</a></i> are also notable and popular Icelanders' sagas.</p>
<p>A translation of the Bible was published in the 16th century. Important compositions since the 15th to the 19th century include sacred verse, most famously the <a href="/wiki/Passion_Hymns" title="Passion Hymns">Passion Hymns</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hallgr%C3%ADmur_P%C3%A9tursson" title="Hallgrímur Pétursson">Hallgrímur Pétursson</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADmur" title="Rímur">rímur</a></i>, rhyming epic poems. Originating in the 14th century, <i>rímur</i> were popular into the 19th century, when the development of new literary forms was provoked by the influential, <a href="/wiki/Romantic_nationalism" title="Romantic nationalism">National-Romantic</a> writer <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3nas_Hallgr%C3%ADmsson" title="Jónas Hallgrímsson">Jónas Hallgrímsson</a>. In recent times, Iceland has produced many great writers, the best-known of whom is arguably <a href="/wiki/Halld%C3%B3r_Laxness" title="Halldór Laxness">Halldór Laxness</a>, who received the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize in Literature</a> in 1955 (the only Icelander to win a Nobel Prize thus far). <a href="/wiki/Steinn_Steinarr" title="Steinn Steinarr">Steinn Steinarr</a> was an influential modernist poet during the early 20th century who remains popular.</p>
<p>Icelanders are avid consumers of literature, with the highest number of bookstores per capita in the world. For its size, Iceland imports and translates more international literature than any other nation.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated60_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated60-194">[194]</a></sup> Iceland also has the highest per capita publication of books and magazines,<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> and around 10% of the population will publish a book in their lifetimes.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_art" title="Icelandic art">Icelandic art</a></div>
<p>The distinctive rendition of the Icelandic landscape by its painters can be linked to nationalism and the movement for <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iceland#Home_rule_and_sovereignty" title="History of Iceland">home rule and independence</a>, which was very active in the mid-19th century.</p>
<p>Contemporary Icelandic painting is typically traced to the work of <a href="/wiki/%C3%9E%C3%B3rarinn_%C3%9Eorl%C3%A1ksson" class="mw-redirect" title="Þórarinn Þorláksson">Þórarinn Þorláksson</a>, who, following formal training in art in the 1890s in <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a>, returned to Iceland to paint and exhibit works from 1900 to his death in 1924, almost exclusively portraying the Icelandic landscape. Several other Icelandic men and women artists studied at <a href="/wiki/Royal_Danish_Academy_of_Fine_Arts" title="Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts">Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts</a> at that time, including <a href="/wiki/%C3%81sgr%C3%ADmur_J%C3%B3nsson" title="Ásgrímur Jónsson">Ásgrímur Jónsson</a>, who together with Þórarinn created a distinctive portrayal of Iceland's landscape in a romantic naturalistic style. Other landscape artists quickly followed in the footsteps of Þórarinn and Ásgrímur. These included <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3hannes_Sveinsson_Kjarval" title="Jóhannes Sveinsson Kjarval">Jóhannes Kjarval</a> and <a href="/wiki/J%C3%BAl%C3%ADana_Sveinsd%C3%B3ttir" title="Júlíana Sveinsdóttir">Júlíana Sveinsdóttir</a>. Kjarval in particular is noted for the distinct techniques in the application of paint that he developed in a concerted effort to render the characteristic <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_rock" title="Volcanic rock">volcanic rock</a> that dominates the Icelandic environment. <a href="/wiki/Einar_H%C3%A1konarson" title="Einar Hákonarson">Einar Hákonarson</a> is an expressionistic and figurative painter who by some is considered to have brought the figure back into Icelandic painting. In the 1980s, many Icelandic artists worked with the subject of the new painting in their work.</p>
<p>In the recent years artistic practice has multiplied, and the Icelandic art scene has become a setting for many large scale projects and exhibitions. The artist run gallery space Kling og Bang, members of which later ran the studio complex and exhibition venue Klink og Bank, has been a significant part of the trend of self-organised spaces, exhibitions and projects.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Living_Art_Museum" title="The Living Art Museum">The Living Art Museum</a>, Reykjavík Municipal Art Museum, <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk_Art_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Reykjavík Art Museum">Reykjavík Art Museum</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Iceland" title="National Gallery of Iceland">National Gallery of Iceland</a> are the larger, more established institutions, curating shows and festivals.</p>

</center>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Iceland" title="Music of Iceland">Music of Iceland</a></div>
<p>Much Icelandic music is related to <a href="/wiki/Nordic_music" class="mw-redirect" title="Nordic music">Nordic music</a>, and includes vibrant <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk</a> and pop traditions, medieval music group <a href="/wiki/Voces_Thules" title="Voces Thules">Voces Thules</a>, alternative and indie rock bands <a href="/wiki/The_Sugarcubes" title="The Sugarcubes">The Sugarcubes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Of_Monsters_and_Men" title="Of Monsters and Men">Of Monsters and Men</a>, jazz fusion band <a href="/wiki/Mezzoforte_(band)" title="Mezzoforte (band)">Mezzoforte</a>, musicians <a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B6rk" title="Björk">Björk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emil%C3%ADana_Torrini" title="Emilíana Torrini">Emilíana Torrini</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Post-rock" title="Post-rock">post-rock</a> band <a href="/wiki/Sigur_R%C3%B3s" title="Sigur Rós">Sigur Rós</a>. The <a href="/wiki/National_anthem" title="National anthem">national anthem</a> of Iceland is <i><a href="/wiki/Lofs%C3%B6ngur" title="Lofsöngur">Lofsöngur</a></i>, written by <a href="/wiki/Matth%C3%ADas_Jochumsson" title="Matthías Jochumsson">Matthías Jochumsson</a>, with music by <a href="/wiki/Sveinbj%C3%B6rn_Sveinbj%C3%B6rnsson" title="Sveinbjörn Sveinbjörnsson">Sveinbjörn Sveinbjörnsson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bj%C3%B6rk_-_Hurricane_Festival.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Bj%C3%B6rk_-_Hurricane_Festival.jpg/220px-Bj%C3%B6rk_-_Hurricane_Festival.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Bj%C3%B6rk_-_Hurricane_Festival.jpg/330px-Bj%C3%B6rk_-_Hurricane_Festival.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Bj%C3%B6rk_-_Hurricane_Festival.jpg/440px-Bj%C3%B6rk_-_Hurricane_Festival.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="756" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bj%C3%B6rk_-_Hurricane_Festival.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Musician <a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B6rk" title="Björk">Björk</a>, the best-known Icelandic musician</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Traditional Icelandic music is strongly religious. Hymns, both religious and secular, are a particularly well-developed form of music, due to the scarcity of musical instruments throughout much of Iceland's history. Hallgrímur Pétursson wrote many Protestant hymns in the 17th century. Icelandic music was modernised in the 19th century, when Magnús Stephensen brought pipe organs, which were followed by <a href="/wiki/Pump_organ" title="Pump organ">harmoniums</a>. Other vital traditions of Icelandic music are epic alliterative and rhyming ballads called rímur. Rímur are epic tales, usually <a href="/wiki/A_cappella" title="A cappella">a cappella</a>, which can be traced back to <a href="/wiki/Skald" title="Skald">skaldic</a> poetry, using complex metaphors and elaborate rhyme schemes.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> The best known rímur poet of the 19th century was <a href="/wiki/Sigur%C3%B0ur_Brei%C3%B0fj%C3%B6r%C3%B0" title="Sigurður Breiðfjörð">Sigurður Breiðfjörð</a> (1798–1846). A modern revitalisation of the tradition began in 1929 with the formation of Iðunn.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="What is Iðunn? Since it began in 1929, it must be something different from the goddess Iðunn. (October 2012)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Icelandic contemporary music consists of a big group of bands, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Pop-rock" class="mw-redirect" title="Pop-rock">pop-rock</a> groups such as <a href="/wiki/Bang_Gang" title="Bang Gang">Bang Gang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quarashi" title="Quarashi">Quarashi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amiina" title="Amiina">Amiina</a> to solo ballad singers like <a href="/wiki/Bubbi_Morthens" title="Bubbi Morthens">Bubbi Morthens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Megas" title="Megas">Megas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B6rgvin_Halld%C3%B3rsson" title="Björgvin Halldórsson">Björgvin Halldórsson</a>. <a href="/wiki/Independent_music" title="Independent music">Independent music</a> is very strong in Iceland, with bands such as <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BAm" title="Múm">múm</a>, The Sugarcubes, <a href="/wiki/HAM_(band)" title="HAM (band)">HAM</a>, <a href="/wiki/Of_Monsters_and_Men" title="Of Monsters and Men">Of Monsters and Men</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sigur_R%C3%B3s" title="Sigur Rós">Sigur Rós</a>, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B3ley" title="Sóley">Sóley</a> and Viking metal band <a href="/wiki/Sk%C3%A1lm%C3%B6ld" title="Skálmöld">Skálmöld</a>, as well as solo artists <a href="/wiki/Emil%C3%ADana_Torrini" title="Emilíana Torrini">Emilíana Torrini</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mugison" title="Mugison">Mugison</a>.</p>
<p>Some Icelandic jazz musicians and jazz bands have earned a reputation outside Iceland. Perhaps best known is the jazz fusion band Mezzoforte and Los Angeles-based jazz vocalist <a href="/wiki/Anna_Mj%C3%B6ll" title="Anna Mjöll">Anna Mjöll</a>. Many Icelandic artists and bands have enjoyed international success, most notably Björk and Sigur Rós but also Quarashi, <a href="/wiki/Hera_Hjartard%C3%B3ttir" title="Hera Hjartardóttir">Hera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ampop" title="Ampop">Ampop</a>, <a href="/wiki/M%C3%ADnus" title="Mínus">Mínus</a> and múm. The main music festival is arguably <a href="/wiki/Iceland_Airwaves" title="Iceland Airwaves">Iceland Airwaves</a>, an annual event on the Icelandic music scene, where Icelandic bands along with foreign ones play in the clubs of Reykjavík for a week. <a href="/wiki/Electronic_music" title="Electronic music">Electronic musicians</a> include ones such as Thor and <a href="/wiki/GusGus" title="GusGus">GusGus</a>.</p>
<p>Among Iceland's best-known classical composers are <a href="/wiki/Dan%C3%ADel_Bjarnason" title="Daníel Bjarnason">Daníel Bjarnason</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anna_S._%C3%9Eorvaldsd%C3%B3ttir" title="Anna S. Þorvaldsdóttir">Anna S. Þorvaldsdóttir</a> (Anna Thorvaldsdottir), who in 2012 received the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Council_Music_Prize" title="Nordic Council Music Prize">Nordic Council Music Prize</a> and in 2015 was chosen as the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Philharmonic" title="New York Philharmonic">New York Philharmonic</a>'s Kravis Emerging Composer, an honor that includes a $50,000 cash prize and a commission to write a composition for the orchestra; she is the second recipient.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Baltasar_Kormakur.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Baltasar_Kormakur.jpg/220px-Baltasar_Kormakur.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Baltasar_Kormakur.jpg/330px-Baltasar_Kormakur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Baltasar_Kormakur.jpg/440px-Baltasar_Kormakur.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Baltasar_Kormakur.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Icelandic director <a href="/wiki/Baltasar_Korm%C3%A1kur" title="Baltasar Kormákur">Baltasar Kormákur</a>, best known for the films <i><a href="/wiki/101_Reykjav%C3%ADk" title="101 Reykjavík">101 Reykjavík</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Jar_City_(film)" title="Jar City (film)">Jar City</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Contraband_(2012_film)" title="Contraband (2012 film)"><i>Contraband</i></a>, and television series <i><a href="/wiki/Trapped_(Icelandic_TV_series)" title="Trapped (Icelandic TV series)">Trapped</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Media_of_Iceland" title="Media of Iceland">Media of Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Iceland" title="Cinema of Iceland">Cinema of Iceland</a></div>
<p>Iceland's largest television stations are the state-run <a href="/wiki/Sj%C3%B3nvarpi%C3%B0" class="mw-redirect" title="Sjónvarpið">Sjónvarpið</a> and the privately owned <a href="/wiki/St%C3%B6%C3%B0_2" title="Stöð 2">Stöð 2</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skj%C3%A1rEinn" class="mw-redirect" title="SkjárEinn">SkjárEinn</a>. Smaller stations exist, many of them local. Radio is broadcast throughout the country, including some parts of the interior. The main radio stations are <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A1s_1" title="Rás 1">Rás 1</a>, <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A1s_2" title="Rás 2">Rás 2</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=X-i%C3%B0_977&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="X-ið 977 (page does not exist)">X-ið 977</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bylgjan" title="Bylgjan">Bylgjan</a> and <a href="/wiki/FM957" class="mw-redirect" title="FM957">FM957</a>. The daily newspapers are <a href="/wiki/Morgunbla%C3%B0i%C3%B0" title="Morgunblaðið">Morgunblaðið</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9ttabla%C3%B0i%C3%B0" title="Fréttablaðið">Fréttablaðið</a>. The most popular websites are the news sites <a href="/wiki/V%C3%ADsir" class="mw-redirect" title="Vísir">Vísir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mbl.is" class="mw-redirect" title="Mbl.is">Mbl.is</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup></p>
<p>Iceland is home to <i><a href="/wiki/LazyTown" title="LazyTown">LazyTown</a></i> (Icelandic: <i>Latibær</i>), a children's television programme created by <a href="/wiki/Magn%C3%BAs_Scheving" title="Magnús Scheving">Magnús Scheving</a>. It has become a very popular programme for children and adults and is shown in over 100 countries, including the UK, the Americas and Sweden.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> The <i>LazyTown</i> studios are located in <a href="/wiki/Gar%C3%B0ab%C3%A6r" title="Garðabær">Garðabær</a>. The 2015 television crime series <i><a href="/wiki/Trapped_(Icelandic_TV_series)" title="Trapped (Icelandic TV series)">Trapped</a></i> aired in the UK on BBC4 in February and March 2016, to critical acclaim and according to the Guardian "the unlikeliest TV hit of the year".<sup id="cite_ref-guardian_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-206">[206]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1992 the Icelandic film industry achieved its greatest recognition hitherto, when <a href="/wiki/Fri%C3%B0rik_%C3%9E%C3%B3r_Fri%C3%B0riksson" title="Friðrik Þór Friðriksson">Friðrik Þór Friðriksson</a> was nominated for the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Foreign_Language_Film" title="Academy Award for Best Foreign Language Film">Academy Award for Best Foreign Language Film</a> for his film, <i><a href="/wiki/Children_of_Nature" title="Children of Nature">Children of Nature</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> It features the story of an old man who is unnable to continue running his farm in the countryside and after being unwelcomed in his daughter and father-in-law house in the town, is put in a home for the elderly. There, he meets and old girlfriend of his youth and they both begin a journey through the wilds of Iceland to die together. This is the only Icelandic movie to haver ever been nominated for an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Oscars1992_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oscars1992-208">[208]</a></sup></p>
<p>Singer-songwriter <a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B6rk" title="Björk">Björk</a> received international acclaim for her starring role in the Danish musical drama <i><a href="/wiki/Dancer_in_the_Dark" title="Dancer in the Dark">Dancer in the Dark</a></i> directed by <a href="/wiki/Lars_von_Trier" title="Lars von Trier">Lars von Trier</a>, in which she plays Selma Ježková, a factory worker who struggles to pay for her son's eye operation. The film premiered at the <a href="/wiki/2000_Cannes_Film_Festival" title="2000 Cannes Film Festival">2000 Cannes Film Festival</a>, where she won the Best Actress Award. The movie also led Björk to nominations for <a href="/wiki/Best_Original_Song" class="mw-redirect" title="Best Original Song">Best Original Song</a> at the <a href="/wiki/73rd_Academy_Awards" title="73rd Academy Awards">73rd Academy Awards</a>, with the song <i><a href="/wiki/I%27ve_Seen_It_All" title="I've Seen It All">I've Seen It All</a></i> and for a <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Award_for_Best_Actress_in_a_Motion_Picture_-_Drama" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Globe Award for Best Actress in a Motion Picture - Drama">Golden Globe Award for Best Actress in a Motion Picture - Drama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup></p>
<p>Guðrún S. Gísladóttir, who is Icelandic, played one of the major roles in Russian filmmaker <a href="/wiki/Andrei_Tarkovsky" title="Andrei Tarkovsky">Andrei Tarkovsky</a>'s 1986 film, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sacrifice" title="The Sacrifice">The Sacrifice</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Anita_Briem" title="Anita Briem">Anita Briem</a>, known for her performance in <a href="/wiki/Showtime_(TV_channel)" class="mw-redirect" title="Showtime (TV channel)">Showtime</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tudors" title="The Tudors">The Tudors</a></i>, is also Icelandic. Briem starred in the 2008 film <i><a href="/wiki/Journey_to_the_Center_of_the_Earth_(2008_Hollywood_film)" class="mw-redirect" title="Journey to the Center of the Earth (2008 Hollywood film)">Journey to the Center of the Earth</a></i>, which shot scenes in Iceland. The 2002 <a href="/wiki/James_Bond" title="James Bond">James Bond</a> movie <i><a href="/wiki/Die_Another_Day" title="Die Another Day">Die Another Day</a></i> is set for a large-part in Iceland. <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Nolan" title="Christopher Nolan">Christopher Nolan</a>'s 2014 film, <i><a href="/wiki/Interstellar_(film)" title="Interstellar (film)">Interstellar</a></i> was also filmed in Iceland for some of its scenes.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 17 June 2010, the parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Modern_Media_Initiative" title="Icelandic Modern Media Initiative">Icelandic Modern Media Initiative</a>, a resolution proposing greater protection of free speech rights and the identity of journalists and whistle-blowers, the strongest journalist protection law in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> According to a 2011 report by <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a>, Iceland is one of the highest ranked countries in press freedom.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/CCP_Games" title="CCP Games">CCP Games</a>, developers of the critically acclaimed EVE Online and Dust 514, is headquartered in Reykjavík. CCP Games hosts the third most populated <a href="/wiki/Massively_multiplayer_online_game" title="Massively multiplayer online game">MMO</a> in the world, which also has the largest total game area for an online game.</p>
<p>Iceland has a highly developed <a href="/wiki/Cyberculture" title="Cyberculture">internet culture</a>, with around 95% of the population having internet access, the highest proportion in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> Iceland ranked 12th in the World Economic Forum's 2009–2010 <a href="/wiki/Network_Readiness_Index" class="mw-redirect" title="Network Readiness Index">Network Readiness Index</a>, which measures a country's ability to competitively exploit communications technology.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Telecommunication_Union" title="International Telecommunication Union">United Nations International Telecommunication Union</a> ranks the country 3rd in its development of information and communications technology, having moved up four places between 2008 and 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> In February 2013 the country (ministry of the interior) was researching possible methods to protect children in regards to Internet pornography, claiming that pornography online is a threat to children as it supports child slavery and abuse. Strong voices within the community expressed concerns with this, stating that it is impossible to block access to pornography without compromising the freedom of speech.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_cuisine" title="Icelandic cuisine">Icelandic cuisine</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Eorramatur" title="Þorramatur">Þorramatur</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thorramatur.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Thorramatur.jpg/220px-Thorramatur.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Thorramatur.jpg/330px-Thorramatur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Thorramatur.jpg/440px-Thorramatur.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thorramatur.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A typical <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Eorramatur" title="Þorramatur">Þorramatur</a> assortment</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Much of Iceland's cuisine is based on fish, <a href="/wiki/Lamb_and_mutton" title="Lamb and mutton">lamb</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dairy_product" title="Dairy product">dairy products</a>, with little to no utilization of herbs or spices. Due to the island's climate, fruits and vegetables are not generally a component of traditional dishes, although the use of greenhouses has made them more common in contemporary food. <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Eorramatur" title="Þorramatur">Þorramatur</a> is a selection of traditional cuisine consisting of many dishes, and is usually consumed around the month of <a href="/wiki/Month#Germanic_calendar" title="Month">Þorri</a>, which begins on the first Friday after 19 January. Traditional dishes also include <a href="/wiki/Skyr" title="Skyr">skyr</a>, <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A1karl" title="Hákarl">hákarl</a> (cured shark), cured ram, singed sheep heads, and <a href="/wiki/Black_pudding" title="Black pudding">black pudding</a>. <a href="/wiki/Puffin" title="Puffin">Puffin</a> is considered a local delicacy that is often prepared through broiling.</p>
<p>Breakfast usually consists of pancakes, cereal, fruit, and coffee, while lunch may take the form of a <a href="/wiki/Sm%C3%B6rg%C3%A5sbord" title="Smörgåsbord">smörgåsbord</a>. The main meal of the day for most Icelanders is dinner, which usually involves fish or lamb as the main course. Seafood is central to most Icelandic cooking, particularly <a href="/wiki/Cod" title="Cod">cod</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haddock" title="Haddock">haddock</a> but also <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herring</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Halibut" title="Halibut">halibut</a>. It is often prepared in a wide variety of ways, either smoked, pickled, boiled, or dried. Lamb is by far the most common meat, and it tends to be either smoke-cured (known as <i><a href="/wiki/Hangikj%C3%B6t" title="Hangikjöt">hangikjöt</a></i>) or salt-preserved (<i>saltkjöt</i>). Many older dishes make use of every part of the sheep, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Sl%C3%A1tur" title="Slátur">slátur</a></i>, which consists of <a href="/wiki/Offal" title="Offal">offal</a> (internal organs and entrails) minced together with blood and served in sheep stomach. Additionally, boiled or mashed potatoes, pickled cabbage, green beans, and rye bread are prevalent side dishes.</p>
<p>Coffee is a popular beverage in Iceland, and is drunk at breakfast, after meals, and with a light snack in mid-afternoon. <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> is also widely consumed, to the extent that the country is said to have one of the highest per capita consumption rates in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> Iceland's signature alcoholic beverage is <i><a href="/wiki/Brenniv%C3%ADn" title="Brennivín">Brennivín</a></i> (literally "burnt (i.e. distilled) wine"), which is similar to Scandinavian <a href="/wiki/Akvavit" title="Akvavit">akvavit</a>. It is a type of <a href="/wiki/Vodka" title="Vodka">vodka</a> made from distilled potatoes and flavoured with either <a href="/wiki/Caraway" title="Caraway">caraway</a> seeds or <a href="/wiki/Angelica" title="Angelica">angelica</a>. Its potency has earned it the nickname <i>svarti dauði</i> ("Black Death").</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Iceland" title="Sport in Iceland">Sport in Iceland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iceland%27s_Olympic_Handball_Team,_Beijing_2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Iceland%27s_Olympic_Handball_Team%2C_Beijing_2008.jpg/220px-Iceland%27s_Olympic_Handball_Team%2C_Beijing_2008.jpg" width="220" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Iceland%27s_Olympic_Handball_Team%2C_Beijing_2008.jpg/330px-Iceland%27s_Olympic_Handball_Team%2C_Beijing_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Iceland%27s_Olympic_Handball_Team%2C_Beijing_2008.jpg/440px-Iceland%27s_Olympic_Handball_Team%2C_Beijing_2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2203" data-file-height="1261" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iceland%27s_Olympic_Handball_Team,_Beijing_2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Iceland_national_handball_team" title="Iceland national handball team">Iceland national handball team</a> (pictured) <a href="/wiki/Handball_at_the_2008_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Men%27s_tournament" title="Handball at the 2008 Summer Olympics – Men's tournament">won the silver medal</a> at the <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Olympics" title="2008 Summer Olympics">2008 Summer Olympics</a>. <a href="/wiki/Team_handball" class="mw-redirect" title="Team handball">Handball</a> is considered Iceland's national sport.<sup id="cite_ref-handball_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handball-220">[220]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sport is an important part of Icelandic culture, as the population is generally quite active.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> The main traditional sport in Iceland is <i><a href="/wiki/Gl%C3%ADma" class="mw-redirect" title="Glíma">Glíma</a></i>, a form of <a href="/wiki/Wrestling" title="Wrestling">wrestling</a> thought to have originated in medieval times.</p>
<p>Popular sports include association football, <a href="/wiki/Track_and_field" title="Track and field">track and field</a>, <a href="/wiki/Team_handball" class="mw-redirect" title="Team handball">handball</a> and <a href="/wiki/Basketball" title="Basketball">basketball</a>. Handball is often referred to as the national sport,<sup id="cite_ref-handball_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handball-220">[220]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Iceland_national_handball_team" title="Iceland national handball team">Iceland's men's national team</a> is ranked among the top 12 in the world. The <a href="/wiki/Iceland_national_football_team" title="Iceland national football team">Icelandic national football team</a> qualified for the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro_2016" title="UEFA Euro 2016">UEFA European football championship</a> for the first time in 2016 and advanced to the quarter-final to play against France. They defeated England 2–1 in the round of 16, with goals from <a href="/wiki/Ragnar_Sigur%C3%B0sson" title="Ragnar Sigurðsson">Ragnar Sigurðsson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kolbeinn_Sig%C3%BE%C3%B3rsson" title="Kolbeinn Sigþórsson">Kolbeinn Sigþórsson</a>. The Icelandic women's team also excel at football relative to the size of the country, with the <a href="/wiki/Iceland_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Iceland women's national football team">national team</a> ranked 15th by <a href="/wiki/FIFA" title="FIFA">FIFA</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> In 2014 the <a href="/wiki/Iceland_national_basketball_team" title="Iceland national basketball team">Icelandic men's national basketball team</a> qualified into the <a href="/wiki/EuroBasket_2015" title="EuroBasket 2015">EuroBasket 2015</a> for the first time in the country history.</p>
<p>Iceland has excellent conditions for <a href="/wiki/Skiing" title="Skiing">skiing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">fishing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snowboarding" title="Snowboarding">snowboarding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_climbing" title="Ice climbing">ice climbing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rock_climbing" title="Rock climbing">rock climbing</a>, although <a href="/wiki/Mountain_climbing" class="mw-redirect" title="Mountain climbing">mountain climbing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hiking" title="Hiking">hiking</a> are preferred by the general public. Iceland is also a world-class destination for alpine ski touring and <a href="/wiki/Telemark_skiing" title="Telemark skiing">Telemark skiing</a>, with the Troll Peninsula in Northern Iceland being the main centre of activity. Although the country's environment is generally ill-suited for golf, there are nevertheless lots of golf courses throughout the island, and Iceland has a greater percentage of the population playing golf than Scotland with over 17,000 registered golfers out of a population of approximately 300,000.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> Iceland hosts an annual international golf tournament known as the Arctic Open played through the night during the summer solstice at <a href="/wiki/Akureyri_Golf_Club" title="Akureyri Golf Club">Akureyri Golf Club</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-w111_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w111-225">[225]</a></sup> Iceland has also won the most competitions for <a href="/wiki/World%27s_Strongest_Man" title="World's Strongest Man">World's Strongest Man</a>, with eight titles shared evenly between <a href="/wiki/Magn%C3%BAs_Ver_Magn%C3%BAsson" title="Magnús Ver Magnússon">Magnús Ver Magnússon</a> and <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3n_P%C3%A1ll_Sigmarsson" title="Jón Páll Sigmarsson">Jón Páll Sigmarsson</a>. Iceland is also one of the leading countries in <a href="/wiki/Ocean_rowing" title="Ocean rowing">ocean rowing</a>, boasting some of the most significant records in the sport. Icelandic rower <a href="/wiki/Fiann_Paul" title="Fiann Paul">Fiann Paul</a> has claimed overall speed <a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Records</a> for crossing each of the three oceans in a <a href="/wiki/Human-powered_watercraft" title="Human-powered watercraft">man-powered row boat</a>, as well as the notable <a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness</a> title of the first rower to ever hold all three records simultaneously,<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> claiming 6 rowing world records in total for Iceland by 2016.</p>
<p>Swimming is popular in Iceland. Geothermally heated outdoor pools are widespread, and swimming courses are a mandatory part of the national curriculum.<sup id="cite_ref-w111_225-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w111-225">[225]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Horseback_riding" class="mw-redirect" title="Horseback riding">Horseback riding</a>, which was historically the most prevalent form of transportation on the island, remains a common pursuit for many Icelanders.</p>
<p>The oldest sport association in Iceland is the Reykjavík Shooting Association, founded in 1867. Rifle shooting became very popular in the 19th century with the encouragement of politicians and nationalists who were pushing for Icelandic independence. To this day, it remains a significant pastime.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup></p>
<p>Iceland has also produced many chess masters and hosted the historic <a href="/wiki/World_Chess_Championship_1972" title="World Chess Championship 1972">World Chess Championship 1972</a> in Reykjavík during the height of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. As of 2008<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there have been nine Icelandic chess grandmasters, a considerable number given the small size of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bridge_(card_game)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bridge (card game)">Bridge</a> is also popular, with Iceland participating in a number of international tournaments. Iceland won the world bridge championship (the <a href="/wiki/Bermuda_Bowl" title="Bermuda Bowl">Bermuda Bowl</a>) in Yokohama, Japan, in 1991 and took second place (with Sweden) in Hamilton, Bermuda, in 1950.</p>

<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20100125144548/http://www.oecd.org/document/60/0,2340,en_2649_34533_1942460_1_1_1_1,00.html">"OECD Tax Database"</a>. 									<div id="p-personal" role="navigation" class="" aria-labelledby="p-personal-label">
